 SVENSKA LAGARNES ÅLDER OCH UTVECKLINGAR 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 Som man i all ting annat velat leda en Ära af Vår Nations och Vårt Fäderneslands Ålder, så har man icke glömt det i Lagarne. Man har försökt att föra dem tillbaka ända till Zamolxis, en Pythagoras Lärjunge, som halfsjette seculum f. Ch. f. gifvit 5 Schyterne Lagar, och hos dem fått gudomlig dyrkan: andre till Anacharsis, en namnkunnig Schytisk Philosoph, som på Solons tid lefde i Athen, och förde därifrån med sig hem Gre- kernes visdom och Lagar: och ännu andre till Dicenaeus, en Egyptisk Vis, som under Augusti Regering säges hafva sedat lo Schyterne. Äran att hafva en Philosoph till sitt Fäderneslands förste Lagstiftare, i likhet af Solon eller Minos, har förfört de bäste af förra Seclets Forskare i vår Historia. Men deras gissningar och namnhärledningar kunna icke gifva en duglig Sannolikhet. 15 Det som angår Schyterne är altid lika oangående * för oss. De förste Lagar hos Oss, som hos alla Folkslag, voro fria och tillfälliga Ofverenskommelser, Landsseder, de förståndigares Utsagor, folksammankomsters beslut, och framföralt Regentens godtfinnande — hvaraf hos våra Förfäder: Dyrt är Drot- 20 tens ord. Dessa voro först mundteliga. med tiden ristade man dem på Trädskifvor som hos oss, likasom hos Romarne och de mäste Äldre Folkslag, var det första papper. Häraf leda någre ordet Balk; men som ock förklaras nog af sin blotta betydelse: Af- 25 skiljning. De egentliga och vigtigaste Lagarne stiftades på Allhärjar- * Se Stjernhöök. 
 
 
 
 
 472 30 35 40 45 Tingen: mäst af Menigheten; och altid med dess bifall som den förklarade genom det bekanta Thingtak eller vapnegnyet: då däremot Vapnatak eller den af Lagmannen framräkta * Spitsens vidrörande, var Domstolens (: Konunga Nämdens) bifallstekn. Därnäst voro de egentlige Lagarne Prcejudicater, Domshäfder eller hvad man sett vara dömt förut i lika fall. När Magnus Ericson i 14 e Sec. gaf Visbyboarne en Lag, lämnade han Magistraten frihet att tillöka den med sina egna domar. En Lagsamling var icke annat än en Samling af sådana doms- häfder. Dessa gjordes af Lagmännen som i älsta tiderne voro Land- skapshöfdingar, Ofverdomare, LagOrakel. Likväl gingo dessa Domar eller Lagar ofta tillbaka till Konungens räfst, rättare Ting eller Revision, där de rättades genom den magt Konungarne hade att bryta Skocksoknur och Ofsocknur; det vill säja hvad som blifvit dömt förlite eller förmycke. Se Loccenii Lex. Jur: Den älsta Lagsamling Historien nämner är den som i 6 e eller 7e Sec. gjordes av Viger, Lagman i Upland, som för sitt Lag- 50 55 Snille kallades Spa, den vise. Den innehöll blott Uplands Lagar, i 7 Flockar, hvaraf Vigers Flucka: och företogs på K. Ingiald illrådas befallning, som däraf ville göra en allmän Lag för hela landet, för att äfven däri hafva en styrka för sitt öfver- och Envälde. — Detta var i djupa hedendomen: den undergeck sedan beständiga förändringar och tillsatser under de Xne Konungarne, i synnerhet af Eric den heliges, Birger Jarls och Magnus Ladulåses Lagar. — Af hela Vigers Lagsamling anses det enda stycket om K a m p och En v i g h e, som finnes vid * Se Wilde. 
 
 
 
 
 473 den nu så kallade UplandsLagen, och några få strödda ställen, vara qvar. Den därnäst gätna är den såkallade Lumbs Lag, gjord af so Lumber Lagman i Västergöthland, der den ock endast gällde. Han anses för samtidig med Viger Spa: gjorde denna Sam- lingen af sin Ämbetes pligt och utan ordning, eller Konunga bekräftelse, af kringströdda Domshäfder och blandat äldre och nyare stadgar. — Några stycken däraf, säjer man, torde ännu 65 finnas inryckta i den närvarande Västgötha-Lagen. Ingen af dessa Lagsamlingar var almän. Äfven Vigers angick blott Upland. Hvart Landskap hade sin egen: och var därom till den grad svartsjuk, att hvar tillträdande Konung måtte på sin Ericsgata eller annars gifva sin försäkran att ej förändra 70 något i dessa hednisk-Kristeliga Lagrapsodier; ja äfven att icke göra några nya. Likväl hafva de följt den almänna förändringen af tider och seder. De närvarande Landskaps Lagarne likna icke de första. UplandsLagen i synnerhet utgafs aldeles omskapad 1297, under 75 K. Birgers Minderårighet och Torkel Knutsons, den förmente förträffelige Författaren till Konunga Styrelsen, upsigt och styrsel, med ett Företal, som för den tiden är ganska manligt och ädelt. Det är sådan vi nu hafve honom. Så hafva de andra Landskaps-Lagarne tid efter annan under- 80 gått särskilta förbättringar och stadfästelser. Äfven Städerne hade sina egna Lagar, Birkerätter; ty Birke betyder en Köping, en Handelsplats, som Städerne i sitt ur- sprung voro. Först 1347 förehades af K. Magnus Ericsson, Smek, på Riks- 85 dagen i Örebro, ett allmänt Lagvärk för hela Riket. Äfven var ingen ting nödigare. Men Prästernes ouphörliga Jäf emot några stadgar af KyrkoBalken, där deras omåtteliga rättig- heter icke nog gynnades, förekom hela värkan af Konungens anstalt. Det blef utan bekräftelse. Värket utspriddes imedlertid. 90 - 
 
 
 
 
 474 95 100 105 110 115 120 Utan KonungaLags helgd, följdes det ofta. Och det är detta som man kallar MedelLagen hvarom så mycket varit tvistadt bland våre Lärde. Ändtligen 1442 lät K. Christopher af Bäjern, genom ÄrkeB: Ragvald, öfverse och med sin stadfästelse utgifvat. Detta är den första Almänna Svea och Götha Rikes LagBok. Den borde endast tjäna till rättelse i verldsliga Domslut: detta höll Präs- terna i ro. 1608 lät Carl IX trycka den för att * lätta ägandet däraf och förekomma de beständiga Olikheterna: men endast Lands- Lagen. StadsLagen utkom först 1618, genom Gustaf Adolphs befordran. Svåra undersökningar hafva blifvit gjorde öfver detta Lag- värkets första ursprung och fortgång; men ingen ren sannolik- het, mindre någon visshet, gifven. Kännare kalla det ett Monument af Visdom och förädling, och påstå att intet land haft en Lagsamling så tidigt som Vi. — Denna har likväl aldrig varit ensamgällande. Att icke nämna en myckenhet särskilta Konunga stadgar, ansågos, äfven efter dennas stadfästelse och tryckning, de strödda Landskaps Lagarne för Kraftägande. De upsöktes därföre öfver alt, för- klarades och trycktes. (de mäste på Carl XI. kostnad och genom Lagman Hadorph) Af dem är Uplands och Västgötha Lagarne de interessantaste: BjörköaRätten, Visby StadsLag, GothlandsLagarne, Dale- och SkåneLagen, de raraste. Men altsedan Carl IXS tid hade en ny tillämpelig Lagbok varit i fråga. Den gamla var mer än till hälften blifven Onyttig. Tusen politiska, kända och okända hinder hade uppehållit detta nyttiga företagandet. — Ändtligen under K. Fredrics * Förut handskrefven, ägd af få, uplästes Lagen en enda gång om året: och borde den, som annars ville känna Lage n, betala pggr för att få den upläsd. 
 
 
 
 
 475 mera fredliga Regering, och i de Republikanska tänkesättens första värma, inföll denna Perioden. På Riksdagen 1734 ut- arbetades, af en särskild Commission, vår närvarande Lagbok. Den lämnades i prof åt alla Lagvittras granskning. 1736 utgafs den gillad och stadfäst. Man vet hvad förändringar den sedan undergått. — Den skall vara ett Mästerstycke i Stil och in- rättning. Men, om jag medger det förra; huru vill Panegyristen qväfva så många klagande röster, eller borrtdeclamera så många Visas inkast? Nehrman characteriserar på följande sätt våra förnämsta Lagförfattare: Stjernhööks förträffeliga kunskap uti the gamle Författ- ningar. Loccenii och Aurivillii ogemena flit. Rålambs berömda upsåt och stora skicklighet. Lundii grundeliga Vetenskap uti Svenska Lagar och Hand- lingar. Kloots Välmenta tillbud: och Abrahamssons berömvärda Arbete och flit. och jag tillägger: och Nehrmans, sökta, mångkunniga, ännu icke överträffade Arbeten i Vår Lagfarenhet. 125 130 135 140 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 KORTARE ARTIKLAR 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 I. DEN ADERTONDE PSALMEN, v. 1-20, EN LÅT- SÅNG AF DAVID: FRÅN HEBREISKAN OFVERSATT, MED ANMÄRKNINGAR, AF CARL STRIDSBERG, PH. & ART. MAG. FRÅN KGL. TRYCKERIET 8:VO, 1783 Så rart det hittils varit at se Bibeltolkningen handterad med någon sann Hermenevtikens och den ägta Smakens anda; så utmärkt är detta Stycke af dem bägge. Den Heliga Tolkningen har varit et kallt Handtverk, fäst och predikadt i några stumma reglor, och som öfverhufvud aldrig fört tanken längre än til det Grammaticaliskas döda skal: likasom all sann Tolkning ej berodde af Uttydarens skarpa och fria urskillning, fattning af skriftens natur och Charakter, hans öfvade Smak, och känne- dom af österländska productioner! Smak har varit en aldeles okänd nyhet bland dessa Kristeliga Maforetherna, bokstafvens lo helgon, och vantrons dårar: därföre när et stort, fritt och filo- sofiskt Hufvud upstigit, som Grotius, som Michaelis, har det gjort hela Seklers härjningar ibland dem, förstört texter, för- domar, typer, mirakler. Det är ju at beundra at en Olärds blotta Urskillning och noga aktgifvande på sammanhanget och 15 den talandes charakter, ofta gjort uptäkter, och öpnat förstånd, där all den höglärda Barndomen såg intet. Mgr. Stridsberg är en Michaelis Lärjunge: och visserligen sin Mästare ej ovärdig. Ni finner i denna lilla Tolkningen samma Anda, samma förnuftiga frihet, de tydligaste drag af hans 20 rena Smak, och af denna förträffeliga paradoxen: At Namnet af Gudomligt ej kan ursäkta mänskliga fel och Okunnigheter. Jag känner en Svensk Bibeltolkare at jämföra med, men ingen at sätta öfver Magr. Stridsberg. De andre äro antingen Grillare eller Utsväfvare, eller Tablaturens ärliga folk, som 25 aldrig tänkt eller forskat sjelfve. 
 
 
 
 
 II. TIL HERR ALETOPHILOS. ST. P. N:0 208 & 9 Min Herre. Multum latrante Lysisca. Virg. 30 35 40 45 50 Om en skrikande hals vore Sanningens bästa organ; om en styf och pedantisk sjelftilräcklighet vore bevis — så skulle jag tro Er vara skickad af Himmelen at nedslå alla Magistrar på Jorden. Men hvilken skada at en så terribel karl, med ljud af en Åska, skal hafva så liten realitet! Ni har väl sett, M. H., någonstädes ligga dessa Majestätliga toma Oxhufvuden, som lofva Vin, men hafva endast lukten deraf? Jag är hvarken Magister Stridsberg eller hans Panegyrist. Ni kan derföre med någon större godhet tillåta mig bevisa, på hvilken grundfast dumhet edra skäl hvila. 1:0. En besattare olycka kan väl aldrig hända ens dispute- rande Nästa, än at bevisa för mycket. Derpå är följande exempel: at en Magister, med sin dank framför sig, bevisar så häftigt emot bruket af flera ljus på et ställe, at deraf följer at Solen gör oss blinda, och bör bort från Firmamentet. Ni, M. H. vederlägger så Stridsbergska Inrättningen, at det gäller: först emot alla Scholor i veraden: dernäst emot hela den ädla och stora Plan af Upfostran som varit följd af alla up- lysta och berömda Folkslag i gamla verlden och i alla tider. 2:o. Desse Folkslag ville at deras unga Medborgare både skulle tåla luften, och lära at känna de människjor de skulle komma at lefva ihop med. De ville gifva dem et allmänt för- stånd, en allmän vilja; och göra dem småningom skickliga för Samhället; märk detta ord, som icke passar med en oändlighet 
 
 
 
 
 481 små, söta och skröpliga huskräk, eller ponerar den dåligheten at hvar och en upfödes i sin vrå. Up fostran, i sin stora mening, är icke gjord at vara hvars och ens husbekymmer: ty et barn tilhör icke så mycket denna Modern som denna Staten. Derföre blanda Regeringarne sig dermed. Derföre är det en Publik omsorg: Och som man altid lemnat i de visaste och lärdaste Mäns händer. Men nu denna publika och stora omsorg, anförtror man åt en Djäkne, en liten Grammaticans son, som icke väl förstår sig sjelf. Barnen lära at mera känna sin Mors hus, än Samhället. Och denna lilla vettlösa yppighet är redan första steget til en söndrad Stat. 3:o. Om den privata informationen verkeligen dugde för Samhället: så återstår: 1:o bekymret at välja en skickelig In- formator, som är öfver de mästas både tid och förstånd: 2:o harmen och olägenheten at ömsa med odugliga: 3:o kost- naden at belöna en dugelig. Den privata Informationen som nu så utbredt sig, är en verkelig yppighet, och både i sit ursprung och sina följder lik all annan. Landet upfylles af små Lärare, som borde sjelfve lära: och näste graderne äro, at hålla sig hvar och en i sit hus en egen Präst, en egen Läkare, en egen Dommare och änteligen en egen Armee til sit försvar. Dock kan detta icke bestridas dem som det förmå. Det följer endast at om yppigheten är för de Store; så äro alla Publika inrättningar för Nationen. 4:o. Hvad controll har man på en Informator? Huru mycken dumhet eller oart kan han icke i frid ingjuta i sin Discipel? Men all öpen Scholinrättning har en alfvarsam och ständig ordning, något högtidligt, och en Publik både controll och värdighet. Så ädel är den i sin Natur. Om den förfallit; så bör hvar redlig Medborgare icke taga sig en egen Informator, som är 31 Thorild V 55 60 65 70 75 80 85 
 
 
 
 
 482 likaså osäkert och i en del söndrar Staten; utan tänka på des förbättring, eller gynna dem som börja at förbättra. 5:o. Angående hvad man lär eller icke lär i en Publik inrätt- 90 ving: så vet jag endast 1:o at nästan alle store lärde Män i verlden gått igenom Skolarne, ifrån Plato förbi Cicero, til våre egne. 2:o. At mer, än en halfrå Yngling, som läser för maklighet eller nöd, måste den förstå, som gjort undervisning til sin hufvudsak, och just studerar detta ämne. 6:o. Om en Informator är god, så tag den. Om en Schol- 95 inrättning är god; så gynna den. Jag ser aldeles icke hvarföre ej hela verlden skal lefva. Och i närvarande Samhällets til- stånd behöfvas bägge. Min Herre! Ni torde finna at jag är billig. Men Ert sätt at no tala emot Magister Stridsberg är så lågt, at det visar endast en som förstår at skälla för sitt bröd. 1:o. At han sjelf skrifvit sin panegyrik: är en gissning, som har något af Nidingsart, ty den kastar en låg och lättgjord misstanka på alt beröm i hela verlden. Om hans Panegyrist 105 ej duger, så är det likasom Ni ej duger. Magister Stridsberg, såsom hvar ärlig Man, rår, efter min tanke, likaså litet för at denne berömde honom så lifligt, som at Ni smädar honom så dumt. 2:o. Om hans lärare, förmodar hvar hederlig at han tar de no bästa, hvar gemen at han tar de sämsta: döm sjelf hvilket som anstår Er at tänka? De stora lönerna bevisa icke de bästa ämbetsmän: och den fattigaste är i verldens lopp nästan altid den skickligaste. Men är icke Stridsbergska inrättningen publik? Hvem som vil får afhöra: examinationsdagar i ansedde Mäns 115 närvaro anställas. Hvad är då at frukta? 3:o. Luften, Min Herre, är något, som efter nya verldens förnuft i Upfostran, icke bör undflys utan sökas. Både kropp och själ skola göras dugeliga. — Vanartiga människjor kunna 
 
 
 
 
 483 aldrig undvikas: eller huru i Er smak har Ni funnit alla pigor och drängar? Men detta just rättar Educationen. 4:o. Om jag medgifver, efter Ert påstående, at Ni icke skrifver 0 vett; så skrifver Ni åtminstone et slätt vett. — Änteligen, Er långa Erfarenhet. Märk, Min Herre! här gifves tvänne slag: 1:o en Häst erfarenhet: 2:o en tänkande och up- lyst. Den senare finner på en dag hvad den förra icke finner på et seculum. Jag vil icke döma hvad Er är värd, som ej ännu lärt Er första aktningen för Personers merit, och publik anständighet. Så mycket har Ni bevist med Er pedantiska uppenbarelse för Publiken, at jag för min del, om Ni ock hade Methusalems ålder, aldrig ville taga Er til Informator, utan såsom mönster på hvad man icke bör vara. 120 - Pantocrit. 125 130 
 
 
 
 
 III. ANDRA SVARET TIL DEN (ELLER DE) SOM RECENSERADE TRAGEDIEN GENGIS CHAN 135 Nå efter Ni ej kan nedlåta er til at svara mig, så måste jag väl då uphöja mig til at tukta er: i det jag låter Publiken klar- ligen se, först er högfärd, dernäst er okunnighet. Pn° er högfärd. 140 145 150 155 Då frågan är om Vett, är ingen annan rang möjelig, än just den som Vett gifver; och den som förstår mäst, är då den högste och förnämste. Hvaraf följer, at den som har de bästa skäl i ett ämne, är där den höge och förnäme, som har rättighet at mocquera sig öfver den andre. Men jag, å min sida, har för Publiken framlagt Trenne rena Facta med sina skäl: nemligen, At det är rätt och naturligt, at göra en bön til Gud med blödig- het; rätt, at svara den lilla sluga niding, som påstår at ens konungahus ej angår en, med häftighet; rätt, at en Declamation efter noter, sådan som jag tryckt den i mitt förra Svar, är det ägtaste Klockare-Genie, hvars effect skulle blifva åtlöje. Ni åter, som kan nedlåta er til at ha så ynkeligen orätt, Ni är dock för hög til at ha förnuft och skäl: hvilken förvända höghet är mig obegripelig, utan på det sättet, at när Ni recenserar Ni brukar at stå på hufvudet, hvarigenom den effect kan up- komma, at Ni ser alt verkeligen högt nedföre, och alt verke- ligen lågt upåt. Och skulle detta ej så förhålla sig, så ser jag i den fåkunniga storhet, som Ni gör er til med, icke annat än 
 
 
 
 
 485 en Comedi Ni ger oss af er egen person: så som paddans välde och storhet intet annat är, än den luft, hvarmed hon sväller up sig. 2" er Okunnighet. Det är den ägta okunnigheten, at icke kunna se sin egen okunnighet, äfven när den visas åt en. Ni försvarar ännu dessa dumhetens stakar 1 för Declamationen, på hvilka Ni liksom ville ha all natur, all känsla korsfästade; men på hvilka stakar nu er egen lilla Smale hänger, och hängandes varder, til hån och spott. Men detta dumhetens stakverk för något så ömt, så delicat och högt, som Själens ton, har åtminstone den ursäkt för sig, at det varit upfunnit i Frankrike för 40 år sedan och där gjort någon upmärksamhet, tils det vräktes, på den re- flexionen, at sjelfva Musiken hade inga tecken fina nog för Expressionen, utan måste fly til den Spelandes Själ, och Skrifva „affettuoso, grazioso, con espressione etc. Deremot, hvilken bestörtning för oss, at se en så myndig Pädagog, som Ni, vara en sådan Scholgosse i just den konst hvaruti Ni högfärdas, at —) vida ex- Ni anser de triviala tecknen för kort och lång pressivare än noterna: då likväl dessa tecken icke ens angå Orden, utan blotta stafvelserna, ännu mindre angå de tankan, och alraminst känslan eller passionen. Efter dessa edra tecken bör jag Declamera grus och Gud aldeles lika, lugn och hämd aldeles lika, lif och död aldeles lika: dock och men, får samma vigt, som Kung, som Vän: hvart annat ord, och hvarenda Alexandrin, får en och samma ton: hvaraf är klart, at om i det förra påfundet var åtminstone ett Klockare-Genie, så är detta senare rent af en fånighet. Sådane äro våre Domare öfver en konst, som är så oändeligt svår, och i hvilken alt måste tagas utur ens egen Själ! ty inga 1 Se mitt Första Svar. 160 165 170 175 180 185 
 
 
 
 
 486 190 195 200 205 210 215 reglor kunna gifva känsla; då man deremot, med ett lätt recept, kan göra så mycke Versar man behagar, och med lite finesse at stjäla, kan göra dem rätt vackra. Också har erfarenheten ty värr! lärt mig, at Actören kan af Poeterna nästan aldrig annat vinna, än tracasserie; emedan Poeten nästan altid declamerar utur huf vudet, då Actören nästan altid måste declamera utur hjertat. Men för at visa, at Okunnigheten hos den, eller de, som Recensera, icke är en händelse, utan riktigt en Caractere; så skall jag anföra den dom, som sist utkom öfver Theatern, hvarest Hr Hjortsberg och de Broen prisas för at ha sagt Ver- sarne lätt och naturligt; men med följande sluga tillägg: „Dock Alt har sitt undantag; Tartuffe, Misanthrope, och andre Carak- ters-Piecer, Och i allmänhet Comedier af et stort Moraliskt slag, skulle säkert förlorat vid at skrif vas på Prose; och olycklig der den Acteur, som djerides at för mycket dölja Skalden"! Här får vi nu på en gång veta, 1° at goda Piecer gifvas, som icke äro Carakters-piecer, det är på ren svenska, som äro 0-piecer, til exempel, enligt Recensenten, den ifrågavarande: 2° at Rimet, som är onaturligt i små Piecer, är en onaturlighet som är naturlig i stora Piecer, ja, utom hvilken Comedier af ett stort Moraliskt slag skulle säkert förlora, likasom at Rima i otid vore icke allenast förnuftigt, utan äfven Moraliskt och dygdigt: 3° at olycklig der (i sådana piecer) den Acteur, som djerfdes at för mycket dölja Skalden, nemligen Rimet (ty alt det Poetiska i plan, caractere och ideer, är för Acteuren omöjligt att dölja; och derom är ej heller här fråga). I Rimet således ligger Skalden, den lille illfundige skalken, och lurar på folkets Själar! Det är visst, at alt har sitt undantag: men at et så stort undantag från sundt mennisko-förstånd skulle finnas i någon Recensents hufvud, det hade jag aldrig trodt. 
 
 
 
 
 487 Läsa Vers och läsa Rim, är således naturligt i stora och Moraliska Comedier! Det är alt för klart, mine gunstige Läsare, at den som har en sådan dosis af 1m. högfärd, 2° okunnighet, ock är skamlös så snart det behöfves. Omöjligen i denna verlden kan det vara förnuft och skäl, som säges emot en sådan liten envålds-herre! Derföre kan man lita på, at om hans Euclides kunde vara emot honom, skulle han prompt på sin E u c 1 i d e s skrifva, ja låta trycka: „detta är et exempel af dårskap och löjlighet"! och sedan le, så fånigt som förnämt, åt sin egen skamlöshet. — Men den som ej med godo vill nedlåta sig från sin skamlöshets högd, har man många andra tjenliga medel at få ned derifrån. A. J:son De Broen. PS. Efter I, mine Herrar, kände det lilla knep emot Borgerlig Lag, at föra in mitt Första Svar helt och hållet uti er Tidning, för at, om möjeligt, hindra försäljningen deraf; så ger jag er nu, för at spara er ett nytt brott, full rättighet, om Ni har courage, at också införa detta. 220 225 230 235 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 ON THE DIGNITY OF A FREE DEATH WITH A VIEW TO STATE THAT GRAND RIGHT OF MAN BY A DRUID Impavidum ferient ruinae TRANSLATED FROM THE ORIGINAL CELTIC, WITH A PREFACE 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 THESE SACRED LEAVES THE FUNERAL ELOGY OF MINANT DE BRIE ARE DEDICATED TO ALL THAT IS GREAT OF GENIUS AND GREAT OF HEART 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 PREFACE Reader! There is still a Druid in the World. He retains the old majesty both of Sentiments & language. I have found this true MAN. I have heard his wisdom; & felt that the heart is capable of all the sublimity of genius. 5 He wrote this defence for humanity, & its greatness, on the following occasion. In December 1788 arrived at Greenwich a French Officer; of fine attracting figure, noble, pensive. He passed there the day & night. The next morning he send back to London his Girl lo & his valet; then distributed, with a simple, irresistible polit- ness his watch & mony. But that generous Act was his farewell to humanity! He cast a look of feeling to heaven & nature; went slawly into the Park; & shot himself. In the instant people came to, & saw him in his blood — dead. They found 15 upon him a Lettre in French, to all whom it may interest, declaring, "That having found neither peace nor freedom in his father's family, no true honour in his political Nation, no faithfullness in love, he had tasted, abundantly, all the pleasures of life: Of these pleasures he had found that of sleep the most pure & perfect: he therefore hastened into eternal sleep. And, if the ever combating elements, once for a miracle, would unite to renew his existence, he did not wish for that supreme gift." (the signature was:) Minant de Brie. 20 25 
 
 
 
 
 494 0! said I, how it is possible to scorn life, that sweet divine; to hate the light of heaven; to sligt the grand Scenes of Nature! 30 0 Friends, 0 beauty; tear of pity, flame of glory; nay even silvery hair of old age! are you then nothing? was there still a heart to resist your power, a mind too savage or too great for your charms? "No — answered the Druid "it was not Life he scorned, that being in nature as sweet as divine; but it was this System- 35 atical Death — the present existence, with its languor & pains, its fever of too high & too low, its convulsive soarings, its frenzy of opinion, its horrid gloom. It was not the light of heaven he hated, but, in the night of humanity, that ghastly 40 radiance of Kings & Priests: not the grand Scenes of Nature did he slight, but the lying splendors, the lofty mockeries, the immense & dull farce of luxurious art; not friends, but the smiles of a fool or of a devil: not beauty, but the smoothness & seducing glare of serpents, the luscious charms of venality, 45 furies in angelic shape: not the tear of pity, but the groans of misery: not the flame of glory, but that base lustre of an envied livery: not the silvery hair of old age, but the dim & dreary ever falling ruins of human life. — "A noble scorn, a sublime hatred that! And this principle it 50 was. For do you believe that it is in the power af any Man, of any being with a spark af sense, to scorn & hate — life, light, happiness, beauty! no, not the least, the lowest of the fancied devils can that! How clear then that a mind as this — does not choose be- 55 tween Life & Death, which stating makes this simple qvestion an high problem of absurdity; One choice only is possible there. But he chooses between — two Deaths; between the woefull, long, inglorious death of such a life! & the divine lightning of a moment. May he even see that life in error; he crowns with divinity 60 
 
 
 
 
 495 his error. May his whole existence have been low or weak; that instant at least is noble. Greatness lives in the vigor of a great resolution, of a great sacrifice. Of this greatness error can change the form, but never the nature. Thus there is a Heroic vice, or a vice of Heroism, namely, a too violent love of what is juste & grand, a too violent disdain of what is vile & wretch- ed. And this is the reasoning of Nature! But I feel the divine flame. I will retire into the grotto and write the words of Truth." The Druid retired, and wrote on a Free Death. Which piece of Celtic wisdom I now give you translated — from this following reason — A Hercules of Sacred prejudice & high conceit stept newly forward in a Prospectus & offered the World to fight the great decisive battle against Suicide in a guiny's volume, large & learned. He would Prove there its General, its Civil, its Reli- gions guilt; from the autorities of God, of Nature, & of all Nations. Tremendous! thought I. But that this mighty Hero, in the full weight & spiendour of his armour, from the flashings of his zeal, may not be deluded to fight a dreary shade of his fancy, nor any real beauty of nature, instead of his monster; I have thought propre to present him before with this specimen of Celtic sentiment & reasoning. It shall either animate or correct his mind, or both. At least it shall prepare the World for the grand moment of his Herculism. 65 70 75 — O Heaven! said I, upon reading this Prospectus, it is not enough, that through superstition & tyranny, deluded man be now in turns abased to every woe of slavery, & madened up to every ecstatic folly; not enough that this whole existence be made a savage play of desires & anguish; but that even the last noble sentiment of his worth be called a crime! But that is, said I, because the dim reasoner of a Sect can 80 85 90 
 
 
 
 
 496 95 never distinguish between a Suicide & a Free Death. The former is selfmurder, against the sacred principle a man still adores in his heart: the later is self-sacrifice, according to even such a sacred principle of happiness & greatness in his higher mind. The one is fury: the other is Heroism. Exactly as Homicide differs from a noble Self-defence, or its higher degree a mag- nanimous protection, or its highest degree a generous war. no Suicide is in its nature a bold and striking action; &, as every action is a crime or a virtue, fury or heroism from the prin- ciple of the acting mind. But is there in nature existing a prin- ciple for a free death? So our Druid has proved. And let the Hero of the Prospectus answer. The general Principle of Man is — to be happy. That is the 105 autority of Nature & of God. But man is happy only in his feeling: & no man chooses death, who does not feel himself unhappy in living. What a simple effect of the most simple Law of God & Nature! no The Civil great principle approve selfdefence in all its de- grees, against every woe or tyranny, even of society itself. The civil principle, if true, is just the best mean, or mean of means for Happiness: it must then include a Free Death, the grandest of those means: that civil principle being but a solemn repeti- 115 tion of the general. The Religious principle cannot differ from God himself, who made Nature & Man. And, in purest truth, it is not man that creates neither the love of life; nor his disdain of life; it is the irresistible great Law of his being, which Law is divine. Nay, it may be said — as man is nothing without happi- 120 ness, that it is a mere natural justness, and as he is less than nothing without honour or the true grandour of his being, that it is a simple law of dignity thus not to exist. But justness of sense, & true dignity of feeling, are both divine. 
 
 
 
 
 497 As to the autority of Nations — a Free Death was ever either the virtue or the error of great minds; &, in the best ages of mankind, a subject of admiration or of silent awe; allways a tragic sublimity! But what is striking over all is this remark: that when man sacrifices himself; most grandly, it is for his sacred faith, for his love, for his friend, for his country. That is for his — own Happiness. And when he chooseth a free death, is it not still to that same dignity he sacrifices all? from the same principle; with the same grandour; saving in one lightening his happiness for ever. Suffer — every high mind supremely can. But that must be for a bliss so much mor great than the suffering. Otherwise, it is but the vulgar callous virtue of misery & baseness. A mad praise of suffering, against Nature, & which confounds all that is most contemtible with all that is most noble! Simple truth in Nature, is so before God. HE is the first great Prin- ciple of bliss, of grandeur, of beauty: He infinitely opposes misery, woe, deformity. Through a spark of this divinity Man loves a free Death, comes nearer to his Principle in that mo- ment, is at the same time more man & mor divine. 125 130 135 140 145 Thus a Free Death seems to be both the right of Nature, & a divine Law. Yet I confess that almost all the modern instances I know of such a Death, have been the flashings of an error, not truly lightnings of a darkened human grandeur. But still, what an error! 0, if the fiery souls of martyrs, if the heroes, the great men all on the globe, must be judged to that rigour, — farewell, pride & glory of mankind. Before the eternal God of Heaven, I condemn an error! But I believe, that through the vile doctrine of Tyranny, that is, false or basely understood religion, every sense of human greatness is extinct; & that, if it were not so, it should be natural to feel 32 Thorild V 150 155 
 
 
 
 
 498 160 a delight or an awe, even from a noble error; the noblest error being perhaps the only sublimity of man. I finish. The Druid comes out of his grotto, & has written. Reader! behold the Law of Nature in the words of ancient Thruth. Chap. 1. THE AUGUST SUB JECT 165 I adore thee, sweet and sacred HUMANITY! & because thy Rights, so divine, are lost in the dreary clouds of Superstition, & thy flame, as mighty as pure, faints, expires, in this sad bondage of wretched Laws. — I will highten the awfull voice of Thruth, I will seize the immortal Style, and extoll thee. Humanity alas! can now not even kindle in the rap- turous, thrice sweet & thrice holy moment of Love — without 170 175 180 the heavy curse of an endless, gloomy conjugal slavery, without these dull woefull Blessings of Tyranny. Humanity can now not breath the little bliss of divine Life, the little vanishing sweet of Existence — without an im- mensity of royal Luxuries, of Wars, of Loads, of Diseases. They have given us Kings instead of eternal Right; and Priests instead of eternal Truth; and Superstition with dreary grimaces instead of lovely divine Nature. 0 what remained then for thee Humanity? ... Only the august moment of retiring from the horrid scene. But no, not so much! not even this spark of grandeur! not even this spark of happiness! For since Kings have made us villains, now Priests watch us. They struggle, they fight furiously — that all may be born, may live, may die in slavery. They curse for us 
 
 
 
 
 499 the Earth, darken Heaven, inf lame the Abyss. They fill all with stupid dreads, enormous lies & frantic horrors. Away! mad damned witchery of Tyranny and Superstition. Let us read the eternal living Word of God: N a t u r e. Let us see — what is True and what is Just. 185 Chap. 2. VIEW In the very first moment, how stricking! that among all the natural grandeurs of Man, there is none which may not be exelled by the other animals, in this or that manner; as his wavering whimsical Sciences, by their divine unerring Sense modest light of Nature; his dull frantic Laws & petty wretched Virtues, by their constancy & Justness; his Pomp of luxurious brillant miseries, by their happy Simplicity; even Immortality itself, & with an eqvally gay fancy, has been, anciently as now, given to all animals. But still there remains One grandeur, and this, this only, — the Sublimity of a Free D e a t h. Through this, Man rises about but Reality & Fancy; he smiles at Tyrants misery! and soars divinely, and scorns, as it seems, the very Laws of Nature. Stupendous grandeur! sublime Right, given by Nature her- self! I defie thee, Human Reasoning, to find another mor august, mor divine. For Immortality, if fancy could be reason, is not to do, it is to be; & the senseless Atom of dust can be as a Seraph. But through this Sublimity of Free Death Man casts a magnanimous look over the sad plain of his Existence, over the vild ocean of Humanity, over the immense stary fields of Nature, and declares as a God — 1 will, 1 will not. 190 195 200 205 
 
 
 
 
 500 Chap. 3. PRINCIPLE OF MAN 210 This great Principle is: to be Happy. And because happiness is inexpressible, & a low degree may be the full sweet of heaven, & a high degree a horrid wilderness, according to the longing heart, & the flame & grandeur of mind — it is always a better enunciation of this Principle: no t to suffer. 215 220 Without this Principle all animality would vanish, sen- sibility eternally groan, all be misery, all Horror in Nature, nothing beautifull & lovely for G o d. He would be adored in Tartarean gloom & angvish. Therefore no Law is above this L a w: through which Nature breathes, every force has vigor, & all is bright & divine. And the other Laws innummerous are but transient shades of this, trembling for its strength, vanishing for its splendor. — Chap. 4. GRANDEUR OF MAN 225 His Grandeur consists in his Happiness. For without this, all is wretchedness, deformity in Nature, woe, all that inspires disdain & scorning pity. Happiness, as it is the Prin- ciple, the Law; so it is the hight and exellence of M a n. In a blessed mortal smiles sweetly the divine Healthy, the luminous divine Reason, the silent divine Delight of Heart. Happiness is the charm of human being, & the true beauty of God in vived expression. If a Man, in the natural majesty of his Right & Freedom, — 230 
 
 
 
 
 501 suffer — it is for a higher sweet. As what a Hero dares in the flame of Glory, what a Lover ventures in the glow of Love. It is not Suffering, it is a grand or bold Action. It is not pain; it is the fiery charm of Courage. Freely chosen Dangers, grand Passions, even the fire of Wrath, even the flow of tears, the dreary gloom of Sorrows, the brillant bold soarings of Joy — all that, in the instant that is Free, it is the hights or depths or pleasant extent of Human Grandeur: it is congenial to Nature, of whose alldivine Power these Scenes are the sweet, or terrible, always august Wonders. that But forced Pain, true suffering, W retchedness is the shame, ignominy, ruin of the Human Beeing, a deformity in Nature, a villanage! To this, take away the distant smile of Hope, the lovely glance of better Days: and a worthy Man must scorn so unworthy a beeing: through the divine Law of Happiness, through the noble human Grandeur. Let him err. Happily God did not give his grand Mind for Principle your little Slander: but — what he feels, and Nature's eternal Right; and Happiness, and Dignity. And lo! how evident! that, Happiness & dignity, beeing the divine Law of human Mind, & Wretchedness, so glaring an ignominy as woe — that a true M a n, whose existence fails of charm & honour, must nobly disdain a worthless shadow, must center his godlike force, & soar, sublimer than Nature and himself. 235 240 — 'Chap. 5. ANCIENT MA JESTY There were Days, when Man felt his native grandeur. Days, when odd lies, villany & superstition, were not called — our 245 250 255 
 
 
 
 
 502 260 265 270 275 280 285 290 holy Religion: when the dull brillant Pittiness of Kings, their farcial Puff of pride & titles, their immensely whimsical Luxury, their bloody Madnesses — when they, & all these wretched minnikins of Men, would have been the scorn & derision of Earth. They had then yet no blessed villains of Priests; no servility of frantic endless brillant miseries; no eternal Fatality of haughty sumptuous and perfidious, conjugal qveens & sweet Furies; no slavery, no fear. The voice of no prophetic rogue or madman yet rose. It was not said: the King is divine; but M a n is divine: not, this Emperor is a native god, but M a n is a native god. In this sentiment let us, amazed in our fatal littleness, see their grandeur. The C e 1 t s, our august Forefathers, a Nation as old as the World, & even as grand in mind, whose songs too sublime for us we only adore, & the least thought of whose Heroes exhaustes all our soul — these C e l t e s regarded a Free Death as simply a noble disdain & heroic indignation. The ancient Go t h s of Scandia, my native Country, had sacred Rocks, high, steepy, awfull, called ättestupor, Precipices of Rocks, from which, weary of life, they rushed down; & were adored in a Song. — I sat in my earlier youth often on such an august Monument of antiqve human Grandeur; & listened to the eternal Groves around, whispering to my Soul the awe, the sublime sweet of Heroism, & beheld the flowery Vally beneath, its flocks, its musing or enhoing shepherds, be- held the shadow, the least breath of the golden age; and thought how happy Man could be & how wretched he is; & learned the contemt of this glittering Generation of changelings & minims. All the Northern Nations, anciently, looked upon Death on a bed as an ignominy, as the wretched sweet af an old Woman. The Sages of In di a, the Brachmans & Gymnosophists of 
 
 
 
 
 503 Old, held this same grandeur of Sentiment. Witness the Heroic Sophos, who in Athen, & before the eyes of Augustus „that he might not be exposed, he said, to turns of Fortune, & according to the irrmemorial custom of his Nation" tells his Epitaph in burned himself alive solemnely, smiling in the S t r a b o immortal Flames. And the august Piles of India in all ages, where the widowed tearfull Beauties devoted themselves to the fire of their eternal Love. G r e e c e itself, with all its luscious graces & charming levity, felt the divine heroisme of a Free Death. See their noble tales of Heroes. Remember the antick Genius who rushed freely down in the flaming gulf of tremendous Aetna. Lycurgus even chose the grandeur of o Free Death, & let his ashes to be scat- tered in the immense air. And Sappho, Sappho! worthy of immortal Songs & immortal tears. And this young Greek musing so sweetly on Plato's bright fancies of Immortality that he threw himself from a rock in the Sea, to enjoy it. And Socrates. And Z e n o, who founded the School of Fortitude and Grandeur. All Antiqvity held this magnanimous Principle. And Rome received this spark of Heroisme, & rowzed it into a Flame. What an augustness of their Self-Devotions for all; of their Decius. What a shuddering of awe & delight to name C a t o! And Marc Anthony, who taught Victory to kiss Pleasure, & united all that is grand with all that is sweet: from him even Cleopatra, the Qveen of charms, the enchantress of Heroes, learned Magnanimity. And all the Stoic demigods: Jun. Brutus, the last of Romans, & A r r i a, the first of Heroines. Rome was the center of Stoicisme, that is, of Grandeur of Mind. The Roman L a w has these simple, majestic words: Si 295 300 305 310 315 - qvis impatientia doloris, aut taedio vitae, aut morbo, aut furore, aut pudore, mori maluit, non animadvertatur in eum. Digesta Lib. 48. tit. 21. Sen e c a, this gleaming Genius, stupendous 320 325 
 
 
 
 
 504 of thought, admired & adored of every Religion, calls such a Free Death: renunciare Naturae, to give nobly back to Nature its right; & he presents this greatness as the last beam of God to an elevated mind. de Provid. C. 6. P linius, the brillant 330 Wit & happy Consul says, that to rush into Death from a blind impetuosity, (: as Sects & Soldiers & every Mind in Fury:) is common; but to Consider, in the light of true Reason, Life & Death, & thus determine, that — ingentis est animi, is the mark of a Mind, immensely grand & elevated. Lib. 1 ep. 22. 335 Even Marcus Aurelius, the godlike Emperor of human frailties & his vast Monarchy, in his sublime T h o u g h t s says: Go ou t from lif e, if life be loathsome to thee; but go out without plaints & without murmuring. And yet this Emperor was ever adored as the swetest & most virtuous of 340 immortal Men. But all this greatness is only — a Sense of Nature, & of human Majesty disdaining wretchedness & higher than Death. The awfull elevating charm of the Death Songs among all savage Nations, with an heroic gayety sung by the vanqvished 345 in that grand moment — proves, that it is natural for Man to be great. - Chap. 6. TRUE, ETERNAL RIGHT OF MAN. 350 This Right is — to Exist on the Principle of his Nature: that is, on the clear Law of G o d, who with the sparkle of life, kindled in him divine Grandeur. And this Principle is enun- ciated — not to be wretched; not to suffer; to be h a p p y, and this to every sublimity. But, when is Man happy ? When he enjoys all the powers of his Being: Health, Reason 
 
 
 
 
 505 & Joy of what he loves. And he may love, relatively to the grandeur of his mind — Goods or Wealth, Women or Sweet Delights, Friends or Society, the Republic or Glory, Mankind or divine Sublimity. He may be a truly great Man, & love them all: he may be singular, & pursue a certain fun of glory, a certain whim of existance. In every case, the fundations of all this are 1. the total Right of Existance in all its powers & blisses, called Liberty; and because existance is nothing without its Good to enjoy 2. the Right of just Possession, called Propert y; this good may be wealth, delight or honor, and always, every one having these same rights according to the eternal Laws of Humanity. It is glaring, that Liberty & Property found, but do not ex- press, Happiness. This smiles in the constancy & bright degree of Health which gives powers & senses; of Reason, which shows the charms of Life & Nature; of Joy in what every Man loves, according to his native exellence. 355 360 365 370 And howthenisa Man w r e t c h e d? Through every violence or Tyranny on his Being, its powers & its enjoyments. Tyranny is, first, the superstitious Tyranny, the which forbids, darkens, & annihilates interior and priestly Reason & every human grandeur, blesses rogues & hightitled wretches, crowns asses, defies Fools, damns the sweet joys, fills all with stupid horror, and through its magic art supports divinely all wretchedness: supports the Second Civil spendid bloody luxurious Tyranny, the exterior & Kingly. And this destroys perfectly Liberty, Health & every true Joy of Man: this places Happiness in the petty childish glories of luxury & mad titles. This, for a little cheering wealth, gives you 375 — 380 — 385 
 
 
 
 
 506 390 395 400 Wars, Taxes, endless Troubles, & Slavery; for sweet delight of Love, gives you Perfidy, sumptuous Pains, and eternal Slavery; for friendly society, gives you precious Grimaces, high Follies slavery; for Glory, gives you the ridiculous splendor of a Liveryman, pompous sounding Lies and slavery. This, for every bright virtue give you a Dread; and for the divine zeal of Humanity, gives you a flaming Pile. ' These two Tyrannies are called everywhere Religion and Government. And in their vast, enormious, infernally brillant Dominions W retchedness is natal; and even blessed by holy rogues as a grace of Heaven. The most exellent Man, there, may despair of Happiness; may easely be wretched. The immense Slavery of Triffles will load & depress him; the gigantic mad whim of Luxuries in these minims, will give him disgust & scorn; the dull pompous struc- ture will fail & menace ruins; he will behold the great dreadfull Show of miseries, shames, crimes, diseases, blood, horrors. Alas then! W hat to do? 405 410 415 The Man who feels in his breast the spark of Divinity — ought 1. to learn Thruth & Magnanimity: 2. to chuse upon all the Earth a place worthy of him, a place of Happiness. "But, he may not find it; or, he loves this!" Then he ought nobly — 1. to slay the Tyrant: if he will not, 2. disdain his being, - and soar. For how glaring is it not! that "Whatever I have a Right to do for others I have a right to do for myself." But now there are a thousand grand Moments, of which the glory is adored, is celebrated in Songs, when a man has devoted himself for his Love, his Friends, his Country, for Humanity. And what he may have done a thousand times for the Happiness of Others — this he should not have a right to do once for his own! What 
 
 
 
 
 507 a dullness in reasoning! And add then, that all these devotions of so bright eternal glory, for Others, were, in true philosophy, really & in first Principle for his own sacred Self which lives only in what it loves. But it was with a mind — eqvally grand, just & divine. This is Nature. Now let us look on the dreary face of Superstition. 420 Chap. 7. HORROR OR CHRISTIAN SUICIDES. What a Miracle of preposterous Dullness — to shudder piously at the thought of a Noble Free Death, chosen in a true moment of human Grandeur, according to the Divine Law of Happiness; & at the same time to raise an immense System of mutual assasination and eternal base Selfmurdering- in perpetual Hellish Wars; in every vile cruel Slavery for wealth or vanity; in impious sporting hazards with Life; as to send Millions af Men over the Oceans not for the Gospel's sake, but for golddust, thea & arrak; in all luxurious madness; in tartarean Brandies, in brutish Feasts & Raptures; in voluptuous gains; in horrid Diseases; in wordly Cares, & killing anguish of Desires, & Shames & Miseries; in perfidious Iniqvity & burning Hatred; in Follies, Vices & enormous Crimes; in famied Hells & all Horrors. This is the grand Christian S u i c i d e; endless in extent, boundless in abomination. At this you will see no Man startle. This all is thrice blessed by holy rogues. But only name Thruth & Magnanimity; & they will cross themselves almost frightened. They will rush back 425 430 435 440 
 
 
 
 
 508 445 450 455 down into their Fables, their dear lies, their wretchedness, & with new fervor adore Superstition. The Hero of a Free Death only feels his own divine Right of Nature, he touches not a shadow of another's; & is nobly jealous of his dignity, & does but honor to his native Grandeur. But a Christian wretch of Suicide — will always begin with murdering his Brethern through every iniqvity, & chiefly in the grand Kingly Feast of murder, called War; & then himself trough every horrid vice & cruel folly — alas! he murders his divine Reason through every Sectarian whim & impious faith; his Conscience through every contemt of God & Justice in the midst of his holy grimaces; & after all his Soul & Being through Hell, flaming in his fancy. will rise to argue against And such a Wretch of wretches noble Men! the least little raving creature of allbonteous Nature — will oppose all that is august of Antiqvity, & magnanimous of Heart, & grand of Genius! Let us check this dull wicked frenzy, — through the evident eternal Law of Justice: "that a Man's Killing of himself, against his Principles & Conscience is Se lf m u r d e r; but a Man's disdain of Life, in conseqvence of his divine Principle of Happiness & true Grandeur, is but a Free Death noble in its cause, worthy of tears & a melting Heroic Song. — 460 465 — Chap. 8. TO SUFFER. 470 This is the deap Principle of Superstitious Tyranny. Through this, all may be done: imposture, gloom, dreary hell may reign. It is stricking how every Religion expresses his Founder. G o d of Nature sways in universal Life & Beauty; & gave to every Being the Law of his own Mind, the Law of Happi- 
 
 
 
 
 509 ness & Grandeur. Moses, vast of Genius & fierce of spirit, commands: Eye for eye, & tooth for tooth. Odin of the Celts could not receive in his blessed Walhalla but Heroes, who had relieved the weak & slain foes, & had fallen grandly. Orpheus & Solon raised the Honour & godlike charm of Humanity. Mahomed lightened of fiery grandeur. If now, through an eclat, a brillant miracle of mobbish Farce, a W retch should happen to found a Religion — how? he would by plain necessity, be the God of W retchedness, & his first Law would be: to Suffer. This is the reverse of the Law of God, the updown of Happi- ness & Grandeur: as is natural for Superstition & Tyranny. And truly, the adorers of this impious madness, through their horrid Vices, are worthy of their Religion . . . How Christians hold their Law of Suffering, may be seen — from their immense Luxury; their endless most Hellish Wars; their stupid boundless Pride; their delight in Folly & Blood, which raves to all the Ends of the World. Yet, we do not speak of any mobbish Gods of wretchedness. We do speak of the glorious only GOD, living in the eternal Truth of Nature, adorable in immense Life & Beauty, reigning in the Law of his Essence, the Law of every Being, the Law of 475 480 485 490 Happiness & Grandeur. Chap. 9. FORCE OF WRETCHEDNESS. Superstition and Tyranny have 1. mad us wretched: 2. have told us, that wretchedness is our nature: 3. that there is such a Force of a Wretch. thing as — 495 
 
 
 
 
 510 That is, such a thing as to be void of all sense & shame, of every spark of human grandeur. Pretty weil! impostors! For otherwise how could noble Men 500 have been a meek adoring creature of mad Superstition, of dull Laws, of proud bloody dolts for Kings of all Horror? It is too glaring: that all that is called Religion & Govern- ment, generally, is but a dark infernal Plan, titled in every 505 age divine, that glorious Duncess, that Fools & rogues might reign & shine. 0 ye have over-weil inspired us the Force of a W retch, that is Vileness: Look around all the Earth. Are we not, everywhere, cheated by Priests, fleeced by Kings, trampled by Dolts of high 510 tittes? Do they not always give us hell now, & heaven in fancy? Is not, under the dreary Sway of Superstition, is not all — folly, misery, blood & madness? Yet after all, & so mighty is the truth of Nature, there is none, to whom it is not evident: "that the Force to suffer - 515 is the Force of an Ass. Chap. 10. MAGNANIMITY AND COURAGE. 520 Magnanimity is a Man's sense of his true Worth: Courage is a Man's sense of his true Force. The one shines in a Love of all that is grand and noble: the other lightens in a contempt of all that may threaten his Happiness. The first creates the true glory, the second the majestic calm of a noble caracter. 
 
 
 
 
 511 But of all that a Man can wish, or do, Happiness always is the Principle. And then smiles Grandeur. For every true grandeur is but a heightening of Happiness. And that this truth may be evident to your mind — fancy in Heaven, on his throne, — with fallen hands & head, a dull weary God: and then, on a flowering turf in Arcadia, with the garlant of the most beauteous Nymph on his head, a Shepheard, gay, smiling, full of his sweet glory. Is it not clear, that Grandure without Happiness, is only a dead Show, a duncical Boast, and that Happiness, in its very Nature, is a smile of Human grandure, a brillancy? Now, what a supereminent Blockheadism in these Sages of Superstition — to pretend, that the Man, who chuses a Free Death, from a simple noble disdain, has not Courage to go 525 530 535 through Miserie s. Indeed, dull Sires, he has not. He has not the Courage ofaVillain, the Force ofaW retch, the Firm Vileness of ignominy. You repeat — for Superstition is madness, — you repeat your Claim to the glorious Courage of a flogged Dog; the Courage to suffer crawling. And the truth of Nature always answers: Yes, dull Sires! he has not the Magnanimity of a Wretch, the ambition of Shame & misery, he has not the Heroism of a Slave. 540 545 Force, in its Nature, is the same, active or passive. Force in every Man, is the same, to Seize or to Resist. Witness every Hero, & every great Man: The northern Champions sung smiling in Torments: as Ragnar Lodbrok in the English Cave of Aspices. So do all American Savages still. All the S t o i k s contemned eqvally torments & death. And what superstitious dolt can fancy, that Ca to had not force, though not vileness enough, to wear the golden chains of ,Cesar: but he — 550 
 
 
 
 
 512 555 560 found & justly, a more noble sweet, as mor true dignity in retiring from the worthless Scene, in devoting all to Roman Grandure, & soaring as a God to Glory. It is plain — that every true Man could scorn all the tor- ments of the World, if he thought this frantic Heroisme worthy of him; & did not see, that to be freely wretched, is yet more a madness than ignominy. In a veord; he has not the Courage in itself insanity or vileness, to S c o r n the div i n e La w of Happiness & Human Grandure. 565 570 But, who does not perceive, that all this oddness of reason- ing, is but the mad witticisme of Superstition: & the cunning dolts, because they adored a God of wretchedness, wanted in.- deed to establish -- the Glory of infamy & the Courage of vileness and the Splendor & Heroisme to be a wretched slave. This is only to have Nature updown. But on so desperate Terms reigns Superstition. All the honor in the light of Truth, of any Suffering, is this: if it be from necessity, it is no merit, though the high coun- tenance in torments is always noble: if it be for Heaven; it is as to be whipped for money, or as flagellation for a saint glory, & that but a mad bloody trade: if it be for the dread of Hell is the universal grand courage of Superstition, 0 then it is the Heroism of Newgate, to keep fair, because he can not help it. for Truth & Now remains, if it be a free Selfdevotion Humanity; & then, I deny it, it is no Suffering; it is an active Heroism, it is a divine moment of Grandure. This, also, is the bright eterval Truth: "that all Suffering, which is in itself noble, that is, a true value of Happiness & Grandure, — is worthy of Man; but that every other is a Wretchedness, proportionally ignominious to bear, which weakens Life into a worthless Shadow, & must excite disdain. And now, if a Man love so truly grandure, as to contemn his own existence, when it does not answer his high Mind — — 575 — 580 585 
 
 
 
 
 513 he is certainly magnanimous; & if he smiles at death, at the loss of every still glimmering sweet, at the vanishing of all- charming Nature, only for that disdain — he has certainly a grand courage; & if he supports in even this dreary moment such a noble idea of God, of Nature & Man — he is certainly free. But Freedom, Courage & Magnanimity — create the human Majesty. It is impossible in Nature to fancy any Man chusing a Free let him a thousand times, through his own errors, Death have ruined the sweet & glory of his Life — in whom you must not conceive, in that moment, a kindling spark of free- dom, courage & magnanimity. Demand not madly in him the lucid contemplation of a Druid. every grand moment is a — Lightening. But if even this moment should be the only honor of his life, the only glance of his human grandure, — let us drop a tear & adore it. Stupid Words are the Magic of Superstition. They will all bawl: "it is Despair!" 0 blessed dolts! yes. Despair is just, when Hope is gone. But an Heroic Despair, & its grand effect, you will never find in a little Mind of a Slave. Wretches de- spair; & damn impotently, & groan, & kiss their wretchedness, void of every noble spark. And to end at once this inglorious battle with dunces — look well on their Magnanimous Men; behold, contemplate the Heroes of Superstition! all this cloud of ragamuffins, stinkards & tatter-demalions, crawling in the glorious dirt of London! all the hidous, dreary prophets of Bedlam! all the patient blessed Villains, rogues, wretches, slaves of Earth! & these possess, each of them, the shining courage of shame & misery! What a horrid army of Heroes: all not worth one disdaining smile of a noble free Man. 33 Thorild V 590 595 600 605 610 615 
 
 
 
 
 514 620 Away then for ever, vile madness of a dunce, dull glory of wretchedness! Chap. 11. GLANCE OF HUMAN MA JESTY. 625 630 635 The Laws of Superstition are innumerable: The Law of God is this sole, glaring: to be happy, through every grandure, & to every sublimity. We have placed Happiness, Chap. 6, in the brightness of Health, of reason, of every joy congenial to a Man's native excellence: native, for art in Man is a torturing of nature. Such a brightness, & every worth or excellence in Man, I call a grandeur; & human grandure, this Worth eminently. No Man being happy in nothing, nor in unworthiness, it is only in his Grandure how petty it may be, according to the true divine Order. An that this may be seen clearly, — let a shadow fall upon any Man's grandeur, though he possess all, yet it will in- stantly cloud his Happiness. So divine is the Nature of Ma n! As he has but one Law, that of Happiness; so he has but one Virtue. And this Virtue of Nature, this virtue of Happiness, is — Grandure. 640 On the contrary, The Virtue of Superstition, the virtue of Wretchedness, is: Vileness. 
 
 
 
 
 515 And this shines hideously in two other virtues: Humility & patience. Humility is a base stupid Lie, called godliness, to deny every worth & grandure in Man, deny his own true right, deny that this is this, kiss the dust & glory in wretchedness. Patience, in conseqvence, is the woefull resignation to every shame and tyranny: it is the grand Virtue of an Ass. And blessed is the Asinisme in time & eternity, because every rogue or fool with a prophetic grimace, & every imperial bloody dolt, can ride us• Ther is not a tenet in all Superstition, which is not a most impudent Lie. And, by heaven, how, if not through the virtue of baseness & suffering, how should 1 m p o s t u r e reign — in blood and folly! But let Happiness & Grandure be the Law of Man, as it is in that august Will of God, Nature; & a divine spark must kindle in the breast of every Man; & he must disdain even the shadow of misery, must glow in fiery indignation at the very mien of Tyranny. Then, no Priest shall abase his Reason, through a holt' Stupi- dity, called Faith; no King shall abase his divine mind & all native Grandure, through a vile Slavery of dull titles, of farcical spendor, of infernal Wars, of mad luxury, of cares, diseases, and ignominy! He shall not behold, with a mean infamous patience his breathern wretches. Minding his divine Law of Happiness and Grandure, a noble blush shall raise, a lightening of indignation inf lame his heart. 0 sweet heroism of Humanity! terror, terror to you, impostors & Tyrants: Your august seats & golden thrones tremble: idols of wretchedness! behold opening under you a Hell of flaming anger, of darkness & contemt. It is evident, that, as all Misery is the effect of the reigning bloody dullness, of Monarchism & Superstition; so even that particular need of Happiness, & of Reason to understand true 645 650 655 660 665 670 675 
 
 
 
 
 516 happiness, & of noble force to obtain it — which is a common cause of despair & Suicide, in the vivid Mind. Superstition and Monarchism have annihilated every lucid thought of Nature, every grandeur of reason; have on their ruins raised an artificial sso Life of Luxury, Whim, high Follies, vanishing Splendors, sump- tuous, endless, wild Desires; a Life failing every moment, a Life of angvish & despair, & which should naturally end in Suicide, if it were not for the dull artificial help — dread of Hell. 685 690 695 So that this reigning, bloody, splendid Dullness, which damns Suicides — is the very Ca u s e of their Freqvency. A little more of its Reign, & it will be impossible to Life at any rate. All will be exqvisite folly, despair, a genius of crimes, anguish Suicide, & what still shines most but a splendid Beggarism. Yet, though in a State of true Nature, Suicide should be, as all, most just & sublime, — yet, in every, every total need of Happiness & proportionate Grandure, Nature has nothing nobler & higher than a F r e e Death. This, I repeat, in a truly human State, of Nature, where the Grandure of Happiness were the divine reigning Law, as it should be mor just & sublime, so it should be more rare. For Man, returned to Nature & its divine Principle, through this Principle is naturally free, happy, simple, majestic; is naturally a mortal God. — MINANT DE BRIE. 700 0 beam or lightening of ancient Grandure! Sparkle of the divine fire of Heroes! thou excitest a Druid from amidst his gloomy rocks, his grove, his wintry Solitude; thou givest him a glance of Celtic August Days. 
 
 
 
 
 517 Is it possible — & ye flow once more, tears of noble delight — possible that, in this age of servility, of brillant misery & 705 littleness, the least magnanimous spirit should remain; in this age, when their highest word Humanity means the soft melt- ing sensation of a woman, that sacred word, which anciently true Grandure. meant A Hero of Human Sense, a Hero of F r e e D e a t h; & 710 only from a noble disdain of even the sweets & glories of this mimic Life; in his mind free as a mortal God; no mob's hell, no dunce's heaven; purely a Man in his simple grandure — 0 this I adore, as a beam of ancient Majesty. He was not a Son of Druids, higher than Secles, & whose 715 vast mind unites Times & Ages as the Scenes of a Drama. He lived amidst this Generation; that is, he lived — a puppet of existence a fool of vanities, of bright servilities, of treachery in Love. — in Love, in the very Sanctuary of delight, in the center of his happiness. He did not faint away, a Shade of 720 misery; did not crawl a worm of Superstition. He contemned what was not happy & grand. He disdained the race of minims. And soared, in a lightening moment; & rests now in the bosom of divine Nature. Not, o noble reader, not that Man may not still be grand & 725 happy. But then he must learn to contemn — the Splendors of Tyranny, the horrors of Superstition; must learn the druidic august Laws of Nature, the simple Majesty of Man. To restore this, I rose from the icy rocks & eternal groves of Scandia: to restore this, I devote my being. 0 Minant! Should I not have, 730 not even in the glance of hope, this only charm of mortal Life; should this Ocean of folly & wretchedness rage yet higher — O then, the horrid roar on one side, the bright immense Scene of Heaven & Nature on the other, I will consecrate the lighten- 735 ing moment & kindle the last Spark of human Grandure. Farewell, immortal Shade! . . . and we, nobler friends — 
 
 
 
 
 518 740 of Humanity, let us strengten our hearts with this eternal text of high Sentiments, these words of T a c i t u s, the godlike Roman, „Optimos Mortalium altissima cupere" that the best of mortal men love what is most grand.
